 <p><a name="page_103_note_1"></a><a href="#page_103_fr_1">1</a> Pleasures of the eye, ear, nose, tongue, and touch.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_103_note_2"></a><a href="#page_103_fr_2">2</a> Hatthappatta<i>m</i> susâna<i>m</i> ma<i>ññ</i>e, literally, 'one would think a cernetery had (suddenly) come to one's hand.'</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 104------------------------>

Itivuttaka, fol. khi (Phayre MS.): kâmânam eta<i>m</i> nissarana<i>m</i> yad ida<i>m</i> nekkhamma<i>m</i>, rûpânam eta<i>m</i> nissara<i>n</i>a<i>m</i> yad ida<i>m</i> aruppa<i>m</i>.</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 107------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_107"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 107</font></a>

 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 109------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_109"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 109</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_109_note_1"></a><a href="#page_109_fr_1">1</a> The rules about the dress of a Bhikkhu who is going to the village are given in the <i>K</i>ullavagga VIII, 4, 3; 5, 2. Compare Rh. D.'s note on the Mahâ-parinibbâna Sutta V, 45.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 110------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_110"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 110</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_110_note_1"></a><a href="#page_110_fr_1">1</a> According to Subhûti (in Childers's Dictionary) sampavâreti means that the host hands dishes to the guest until the latter says, 'I have had enough.' Childers accordingly translates sampavâreti, 'to cause to refuse.' But as pavâreti means, 'to cause to accept,' it is impossible that sampavâreti should have exactly the opposite meaning. We prefer, therefore, to take it as an emphatic synonym of pavâreti.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 112------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_112"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 112</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_112_note_1"></a><a href="#page_112_fr_1">1</a> This cannot be understood as a general rule, for it is repeated nowhere where precepts for wandering Bhikkhus are given, and, on the contrary, numerous instances occur in the Sacred Texts in which two or more Bhikkhus are mentioned as wandering together, without any expression of disapproval being added. The precept given here evidently is intended to refer only to the earliest period in the spread of the new doctrine; just as in chap. 12 a form of upasampadâ is introduced by Buddha which was regarded as inadmissible in later times.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 113------------------------>

The Lalita Vistara has Senâpatigrâma.</p>

 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 118------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_118"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 118</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_118_note_1"></a><a href="#page_118_fr_1">1</a> The <i>G</i>a<i>t</i>ilas (i.e. ascetics wearing matted hair) are Brahmanical vânaprasthas. The description of their ascetic life given in many passages of the <i>G</i>âtaka Atthava<i>nn</i>anâ and of the Apadâna exactly agrees with the picture of the forest life of the {Greek: <i>ulóbioi</i>} which so frequently occurs in the Mahâbhârata. In the Mahâvagga (VI, 35, 2) it is expressly stated that the <i>G</i>a<i>t</i>ilas recognised the authority of the Veda, and it is in keeping with this that the usual term for adopting the state of a <i>G</i>a<i>t</i>ila is 'isipabba<i>gg</i>am pabba<i>g</i>ati' (frequently in the <i>G</i>ât. Atth.), i.e. leaving the world and becoming a <i>Ri</i>shi.</p>

 <p><a name="page_119_note_2"></a><a href="#page_119_fr_2">2</a> Sati<i>m</i> upa<i>tth</i>âpetvâ. Sati is here a more precise idea than memory.</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 137------------------------>

 <p><a name="page_137_note_1"></a><a href="#page_137_fr_1">1</a> The word householder (gahapati) is used here, as is the case not unfrequently, to denote householders of the third caste. Compare Rh. D.'s' note on Mahâ-sudassana Sutta, <a href="../sbe11/sbe1108.htm#page_260">p. 260</a>.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 138------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_138"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 138</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_138_note_1"></a><a href="#page_138_fr_1">1</a> Literally, 'who is known as emaciate.' This is said with reference to the mortifications practised by the <i>G</i>a<i>t</i>ilas or Vânaprasthas. The Mahâbhârata (III, 1499) uses the same adjective (k<i>ris</i>a) of a <i>G</i>a<i>t</i>ila. Vadâno we take for a participle, but it is possible also to read vadâ no, 'tell us,' which Professor Jacobi (Zeitschrift der Deutschen Morg. Ges., XXXIV, p. 187) prefers. Buddhaghosa takes kisakovadâno for a compound of kisaka and ovadâna: tâpasâna<i>m</i> ovâdako anusâsako.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_138_note_2"></a><a href="#page_138_fr_2">2</a> The meaning is: The mantras which are recited at the sacrifices contain praises of visible things, &amp;c., and the rewards that are promised to him who offers such sacrifices do not extend beyond that same sphere.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_138_note_3"></a><a href="#page_138_fr_3">3</a> The Pâli word is upadhi, which is translated by Childers, 'substratum of being.' See our note on chap. 5. 2. In this passage upadhi is said to refer to the Khandhas (Buddhaghosa).</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_138_note_4"></a><a href="#page_138_fr_4">4</a> Here we have the Vedic distinction of greater and smaller sacrifices (ya<i>g</i>atayas and <i>g</i>uhotaras).</p>

 <p><a name="page_138_note_6"></a><a href="#page_138_fr_6">6</a> Doubtless Buddhaghosa is right in explaining ko by kva.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 139------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_139"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 139</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_139_note_1"></a><a href="#page_139_fr_1">1</a> Aki<i>ñk</i>ana here, and elsewhere, used as an epithet of Arahatship, refers to the state of mind in which the ki<i>ñk</i>anas, that is, lust, malice, and delusion (so in the Sa<i>m</i>gîti Sutta of the Digha Nikâya), have ceased to be. It is literally 'being without the somethings,' which are the things that stand in the way, the obstacles to Buddhist perfection; and Buddhaghosa (in the Suma<u>n</u>gala Vilâsinî on the passage in the Sa<i>m</i>gîti Sutta) explains accordingly ki<i>ñk</i>ana by pa<i>l</i>ibodha.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 141------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_141"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 141</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_141_note_1"></a><a href="#page_141_fr_1">1</a> Gold colour is one (the 17th) of the thirty-two lakkha<i>n</i>a which form the characteristics of Buddha as a mahâpurisa.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_141_note_2"></a><a href="#page_141_fr_2">2</a> The ten ariyavâsas. Buddhaghosa says: dasasu ariyavâsesu vutthavâso. The Sa<i>m</i>gîti Sutta gives the ten Noble States, as follows: 1. being free from the five bad qualities (pa<i>ñk</i>a<u>n</u>ga), 2. being possessed of the six good qualities (<i>kh</i>a<i>l</i>a<u>n</u>ga), 3. being guarded in the one thing (ekârakkha), 4. observing four things (<i>k</i>aturâpassena), 5. rejecting each of the four false truths (panunna pa<i>kk</i>eka-sa<i>kk</i>a), 6. seeking right things (samavayasa-dhesana), 7. having pure aims (anâvila-sa<i>m</i>kappa), 8. being full of ease (passaddhakâya-sa<i>m</i>khâra), 9. being emancipated in heart (suvimutta<i>k</i>itta), 10. being emancipated in ideas (suvimuttapa<i>ññ</i>a). The Sa<i>m</i>gîti then further enlarges on the meaning of each of these ten.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 142------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_142"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 142</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_142_note_1"></a><a href="#page_142_fr_1">1</a> The ten Balas, which are ten kinds of knowledge (<i>ñ</i>â<i>n</i>a); see Bumouf, Lotus, p. 781 and following, and compare <i>G</i>âtaka I, 78.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_142_note_2"></a><a href="#page_142_fr_2">2</a> Buddhaghosa explains dasadhammavidû by dasakammapathavidû.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_142_note_3"></a><a href="#page_142_fr_3">3</a> Buddhaghosa explains dasabhi <i>k</i>' ûpeto by suppJying asekhehi dhammehi. The first eight of the ten asekhâ dhammâ consist in the full perfection of sammâdi<i>tth</i>i (right belief) and the other categories enumerated in the formula of the Noble Eightfold Path; the ninth and tenth are the perfection of sammâ<i>ñ</i>â<i>n</i>a (right knowledge) and sammâvimutti (right emancipation).</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 143------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_143"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 143</font></a>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 145------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_145"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 145</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_145_note_1"></a><a href="#page_145_fr_1">1</a> This seems to us the meaning of atthikehi upa<i>ññ</i>âta<i>m</i> magga<i>m</i>.  <p><a name="page_145_note_2"></a><a href="#page_145_fr_2">2</a> The same words as are put in the mouth of Upaka, when addressing the Buddha, above, chap. 6, § 7 (and see below, § 6).</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 146------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_146"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 146</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_146_note_1"></a><a href="#page_146_fr_1">1</a> This famous stanza doubtless alludes to the formula of the twelve Nidânas (see chap. 1. 2) which explains the origination and cessation of what are called here ' dhammâ hetuppabhavâ.' Hetu and pa<i>kk</i>aya (the word so frequently used in the formula of the Nidânas) are nearly synonymous. Colebrooke (Life and Essays, vol. ii. p. 419) says that the Bauddhas distinguish between hetu, 'proximate cause,' and pa<i>kk</i>aya (pratyaya), 'concurrent occasion;' but, in practical use, this slight difference of meaning, if it really existed, has but little weight attached to it.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 147------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_147"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 147</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_147_note_1"></a><a href="#page_147_fr_1">1</a> See §§ 2-4. lnstead of 'The paribbâ<i>g</i>aka Sâriputta,' of course, the pronoun of the first person is to be read; instead of' The venerable Assa<i>g</i>i , read, 'The Bhikkhu Assa<i>g</i>i;' and further, the vocative 'Friend' (âvuso), addressed to Moggallâna, is inserted three or four times in the course of this narration.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 149------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_149"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 149</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_149_note_1"></a><a href="#page_149_fr_1">1</a> The later Burmese and Chinese works translated by Bigandet (Life of Gaudama, p. 152) and by Beal (Romantic Legend, p. 330) add that he died. This is not in the Pâli text, and the Sinhalese account given by Hardy (Manual, p. 197) is directly opposed to that statement.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_149_note_2"></a><a href="#page_149_fr_2">2</a> Upatissa was called Sâriputta after his mother ('The Son of Sârî'); Kolita had the family name Moggallâna (compare Beal, Romantic Legend, pp. 324, 331). The name Upatissa occurs in Asoka's well-known edict which has been found at Bairât. The king there quotes 'The Question of Upatissa' among the texts, the study of which he recommends to the brethren and sisters of the fraternity and to the laymen of either sex. This very probably refers to the dialogue between Assa<i>g</i>i and Sâriputta.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_149_note_3"></a><a href="#page_149_fr_3">3</a> As to this repetition of what had been related before, comp. the note on chap. 15. 6, 7. The words from gambhîre down to upadhisa<i>m</i>khaye form a <i>s</i>loka. This is one of several instances where an older passage in verse, and probably first composed in some nearly related dialect, appears in the Pâli Pi<i>t</i>akas in prose. It is this which explains the extraordinary grammatical construction of the first seven words. Compare Rh. D.'s note on the similar instance at Mahâ-parinibbâna Sutta <a href="../sbe11/sbe1103.htm#page_106">V, 62</a>. The exclamation put into the mouth of Sâriputta, and afterwards of Moggallâna (above, chap. 23, §§ 5, 10), ought also, perhaps, to be included in the same category.</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 151------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_151"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 151</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_151_note_1"></a><a href="#page_151_fr_1">1</a> The chief object of the first book being to discuss the regulations for the upasampadâ ordination, at which the preceptor (upa<i>ggh</i>âya) of the candidate has a principal part, the text now goes on to relate the institution of the office and upa<i>ggh</i>âyas, and to explain the mutual duties incumbent on upa<i>ggh</i>âyas and pupils (saddhivihârikas).</p>

 <p><a name="page_152_note_1"></a><a href="#page_152_fr_1">1</a> Buddhaghosa has the following note on utti<i>tth</i>apatta: 'utti<i>tth</i>apattan ti pi<i>nd</i>âya <i>k</i>ara<i>n</i>akapatta<i>m</i>, tasmi<i>m</i> hi manussâ u.<i>kk</i>i<i>tth</i>asa<i>ññ</i>ino (this word is spelt so in the Paris MS. as well as in the Berlin MS. of the Samanta Pâsâdikâ; the usual spelling is u<i>kkh</i>i<i>tth</i>a), tasmâ utti<i>tth</i>apattan ti vutta<i>m</i>. athavâ u<i>tth</i>ahitvâ patta<i>m</i> upanâmentîti evam ettha attho da<i>tth</i>abbo.' We take the word, as the former of Buddhaghosa's two explanations implies, for a composition of u<i>kkh</i>i<i>tth</i>a. For the conversion of palatal consonants into dentals, see E. Kuhn, Beiträge zur Pali-Grammatik, p. 36, and on the use of the word compare Trenckner's Milinda Pa<i>ñ</i>ho, pp. 213, 214.</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 166------------------------>

 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_166_note_1"></a><a href="#page_166_fr_1">1</a> Those slight offences which were not embodied in the Pâtimokkha are called dukka<i>t</i>a offences. They range, as to their gravity, with the pâ<i>k</i>ittiya offences of the Pâtimokkha. For him who had committed a dukka<i>t</i>a offence, no further penance was required than a simple confession of his fault. See <i>K</i>ullavagga , XI, 1, 10.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 169------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_169"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 169</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_169_note_2"></a><a href="#page_169_fr_2">2</a> The form for bringing a formal motion before the Order is the following: The mover first announces to the assembled Bhikkhus what resolution he is going to propose; this announcement is called <i>ñ</i>atti (see, for instance, § 4). After the <i>ñ</i>atti follows the question put to the Bhikkhus present if they approve the resolution. This question is put either once or three times; in the first case we have a <i>ñ</i>attidutiya kamma(see, for instance, II, chap. 6); in the second case, a <i>ñ</i>attikatuttha kamma (as in this chapter).</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 173------------------------>

 <p><a name="page_173_note_1"></a><a href="#page_173_fr_1">1</a> These are the five kinds of dwellings (pa<i>ñk</i>a lenâni) which are declared to be allowable, <i>K</i>ullavagga VI, I, 2. The single expressions are explained by Buddhaghosa in his note on <i>K</i>ullavagga 1. 1. as follows: 'a<i>ddh</i>ayogo 'ti suva<i>nn</i>ava<u>n</u>gageha<i>m</i>, pâsâdo 'ti dîghapâsâdo, hammiyan ti upariâkâsatale pati<i>tth</i>itakû<i>t</i>âgâro pâsâdo yeva, gubâ 'ti i<i>tth</i>akaguhâ silâguhâ dâruguhâ pa<i>m</i>suguhâ,' i.e. 'A<i>ddh</i>a-yoga is a gold-coloured Bengal house. Pâsâda is a long storied mansion (or, the whole of an upper storey). Hammiya is a Pâsâda, which has an upper chamber placed on the topmost storey. Gubâ is a hut made of bricks, or in a rock, or of wood.'</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 177------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_177"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 177</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_177_note_1"></a><a href="#page_177_fr_1">1</a> See the conclusion of this in chapter 38.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 178------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_178"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 178</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_178_note_1"></a><a href="#page_178_fr_1">1</a> Buddhaghosa can scarcely be right in explaining pakkhasa<i>m</i>kanta by titthiyapakkhasa<i>m</i>kanta.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_178_note_2"></a><a href="#page_178_fr_2">2</a> Â<i>k</i>ariya as well as upa<i>ggh</i>âya means 'teacher,' or 'preceptor.' It is very difficult or rather impossible to draw a sharp line of distinction between â<i>k</i>ariyâ and upa<i>ggh</i>âya. The duties of an â<i>k</i>ariya towards his antevâsika, and of an antevâsika towards his â<i>k</i>ariya, as indicated in chaps. 32, 33 ( = <i>K</i>ullavagga VIII, 13, 14), are exactly the same as those of an upa<i>ggh</i>âya towards his saddhivihârika and vice versa (chaps. 25, 26 = <i>K</i>ullavagga VIII, 11, 12). The position of an upa<i>ggh</i>âya, however, was considered as the more important of the two; at the upasampadâ service the upa<i>ggh</i>âya had a more prominent part than the â<i>k</i>ariya, as we may infer from chaps. 28, 29, and from the explanations on the 65th pâ<i>k</i>ittiya rule which are given in the Sutta Vibha<u>n</u>ga. There it is said that, if the upasampadâ ordination had been conferred, against the rule, on a person that has not yet attained his twentieth year, the upa<i>ggh</i>âya has made himself guilty of a pâ<i>k</i>ittiya offence, the â<i>k</i>ariya and the other present Bhikkhus only of a dukka<i>t</i>a offence. We may add that the succession of Vinaya teachers from Upâli down to Mahinda, which is given in the Dîpava<i>m</i>sa (Bhâ<i>n</i>avâras IV and V), is a succession of upa<i>ggh</i>âyas and saddhivihârikas (see IV, 36, 42, 43, &amp;c.), not of â<i>k</i>ariyas and antevâsikas; the duty of instructing the young Bhikkhus in the holy doctrines and ordinances seems, therefore, to belong to the upa<i>ggh</i>âya rather than to the â<i>k</i>ariya; compare also Dîpava<i>m</i>sa VII, 26. So among the Brâhma<i>n</i>as, on the contrary, the â<i>k</i>ârya is estimated higher than the upâdhyâya; see Manu II, 145; Yâ<i>gñ</i>avalkya I, 35. Compare also chap. 36. 1 (end of the paragraph), and Buddhaghosa's explanation of that passage.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 179------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_179"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 179</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_179_note_1"></a><a href="#page_179_fr_1">1</a> Nissaya (i.e. dependence) is the relation between â<i>k</i>ariya and antevâsika. The antevâsika lives ' nissâya ' with regard to the â<i>k</i>ariya, i.e. dependent on him; the â<i>k</i>ariya gives his nissaya to the antevâsika. i.e. he receives him into his protection and care. At chap. 36. 1, 'nissaya ' is said also of the relation between upa<i>ggh</i>âya and saddhivihârika.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 181------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_181"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 181</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_181_note_1"></a><a href="#page_181_fr_1">1</a> That is, 'did not know how to decide whether their nissaya was destroyed, or not.'</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 182------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_182"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 182</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_182_note_1"></a><a href="#page_182_fr_1">1</a> This refers, according to Buddhaghosa, to the pa<i>n</i>âmanâ (turning away of the saddhivihârika); see chap. 27. 2.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_182_note_2"></a><a href="#page_182_fr_2">2</a> Buddhaghosa: 'Coming together may be understood either by seeing or by hearing. If a saddhivihârika who lives in dependence (nissâya) on his â<i>k</i>ariya sees his upa<i>ggh</i>âya paying homage to a sacred shrine in the same Vihâra, or going on his rounds in the same village, cessation of the nissaya (towards the â<i>k</i>ariya) is the consequence. If he hears the voice of his upa<i>ggh</i>âya, who preaches the Dhamma or gladdens (lay-people by religious discourse), in the Vihâra or in the interior of a house, and if he recognises that it is his upa<i>ggh</i>âya's voice, cessation of the nissaya (towards the â<i>k</i>ariya) is the consequence.'</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 184------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_184"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 184</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_184_note_1"></a><a href="#page_184_fr_1">1</a> According to Buddhaghosa, moral transgression (adhisîla) is said with regard to offences against the pârâ<i>g</i>ika and sa<i>m</i>ghâdisesa rules, while transgressions in conduct (a<i>ggh</i>â<i>k</i>âra) consist in offences against the minor rules of the Pâtimokkha.  <!------------------------footnotes page 185------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_185"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 185</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_185_note_1"></a><a href="#page_185_fr_1">1</a> This means instructing him in the sekhapa<i>ññ</i>atti (Buddhaghosa). We cannot say what is the accurate meaning of the last term, which apparently, as its verbal meaning seems to imply, refers to ordinances for those Bhikkhus who have entered the path of sanctification, but have not yet attained Arahatship. Spence Hardy (Manual, p. 493) gives the term sekha-sîla, which he explains as the observance of precepts in order to become a sekha. See also Hardy's note on âdibrahmakariya-sila, l. l. p. 492.</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 191------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_191"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 191</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_191_note_1"></a><a href="#page_191_fr_1">1</a> <i>G</i>îvaka was physician to king Bimbisâra, and one of the chief partisans of Buddha at the court of Râgugaha. See VIII, 1, the introduction of the Sâma<i>ññ</i>aphala Sutta, &amp;c.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 194------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_194"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 194</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_194_note_1"></a><a href="#page_194_fr_1">1</a> On u<i>kk</i>inatha, compare the use of u<i>kkh</i>e<i>kkh</i>âmi at Mahâparinibbâna Sutta I, 1 (p. 1), which Buddhaghosa rightly explains by u<i>kkh</i>indissâmi. But we think it better to adhere here to the reading u<i>kk</i>inatha, in accordance with the MSS.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 196------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_196"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 196</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_196_note_1"></a><a href="#page_196_fr_1">1</a> The robber A<u>n</u>gulimâla (i.e. he who wears a necklace of fingers), whose original name was Ahi<i>m</i>saka, had received this surname from his habit of cutting off the fingers of his victims and wearing them as a necklace. See Spence Hardy, Manual, p. 249 seq. {See also <a href="../../journals/oc/gppt4.htm">Majjhima Nikâya, Sutta 86</a>, translated by Albert J. Edmunds.}</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 200------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_200"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 200</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_200_note_1"></a><a href="#page_200_fr_1">1</a> Buddhaghosa explains kammârabha<i>nd</i>u by tulâtaramu<i>nd</i>ako (read tulâdhâram.) suva<i>nn</i>akâraputto. At Dhammapada, v. 239, kammâra is said of a silversmith. There was probably no distinction in these early times between gold, silver, copper, and iron smiths; the same man being an artificer in all kinds of metal.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 201------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_201"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 201</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_201_note_1"></a><a href="#page_201_fr_1">1</a> This Upâli is different from the famous Upâli who belonged to the chief disciples of Buddha; the latter came not from Râ<i>g</i>agaha, but from the Sakya country.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_201_note_2"></a><a href="#page_201_fr_2">2</a> Buddhaghosa: 'He who learns arithmetic, must think much; therefore his breast will become diseased.'</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_201_note_3"></a><a href="#page_201_fr_3">3</a> We prefer this translation of rûpa to translating it by 'painting,' on account of Buddhaghosa's note: 'He who learns the rûpa-sutta must turn over and over many kârshâpa<i>n</i>as and look at them.'</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 203------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_203"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 203</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_203_note_1"></a><a href="#page_203_fr_1">1</a> The law alluded to is the 65th pâ<i>k</i>ittiya rule. Generally in the Khandhakas, which presuppose, as we have stated in our preface, the existence of the Pâtimokkha, direct repetition of the rules laid down there has been avoided. If, nevertheless, in the Khandhakas a transgression alluded to in the Pâtimokkha had to be mentioned again, then in most cases the Khandhakas, instead of directly indicating the penance incurred thereby, use of the guilty Bhikkhu the expression, 'yathâdhammo kâretabbo,' i.e. 'he is to be treated according to the law.' See H. O.'s Introduction to his edition of the Mahâvagga, p. xx note.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 204------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_204"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 204</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_204_note_1"></a><a href="#page_204_fr_1">1</a> Buddhaghosa explains abivâtakaroga by mâribyâdhi, and says: 'When this plague befalls a house, men and beasts in that house die; but he who breaks through wall or roof, or is "rogâ mâdigato (?)," may be saved.'</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 205------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_205"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 205</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_205_note_1"></a><a href="#page_205_fr_1">1</a> This seems very unpractical: and the rule is accordingly practically abrogated again by chapter 55.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_205_note_2"></a><a href="#page_205_fr_2">2</a> We must leave 'âhundarikâ' untranslated; Buddhaghosa says nothing about this obscure word.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 206------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_206"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 206</font></a>

 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 207------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_207"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 207</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_207_note_1"></a><a href="#page_207_fr_1">1</a> Supply these pentads and hexads, respectively, from chaps. 36. 6, 7; 8, 9; 14, 15; 16, 17; 37. 1, 2; 5, 6; 7, 8; 13, 14.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_207_note_2"></a><a href="#page_207_fr_2">2</a> Abhayûvara means, 'secure from anything.' This refers to the expression used in chap. 42, § 2.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 208------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_208"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 208</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_208_note_1"></a><a href="#page_208_fr_1">1</a> The Buddha's former wife. This is, as far as we know, the only passage in the Pâli Pi<i>t</i>akas which mentions this lady, and it deserves notice that her name is not mentioned. Probably this name was unknown to the Buddhists in early times, and thus we may best account for the difference of the simply invented names given to this lady by later writers. Compare Rh. D., Buddhism, p. 50 seq.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 209------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_209"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 209</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_209_note_1"></a><a href="#page_209_fr_1">1</a> Granting a boon (vara) is a constant phrase used of princes when making an open promise to give to any one whatever they should ask. See, for instance, the <i>G</i>âtaka Story, No. 9, where the person to whom the boon was given laid it by for a convenient season; and then asked the king to make her son heir-apparent, in violation of all ancient law and custom.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 210------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_210"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 210</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_210_note_1"></a><a href="#page_210_fr_1">1</a> Nanda was a son of Mahâpa<i>g</i>âpati, a half-brother of the Buddha. See the story of his conversion in Rh. D.'s Buddhist Birth Stories, p. 128 (later and fuller accounts can be seen in Hardy, Manual, p. 204 seq.; Beal, Romantic Legend, p. 369 seq.)</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 211------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_211"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 211</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_211_note_2"></a><a href="#page_211_fr_2">2</a> Sikkhâpadâni, literally, 'Paths of Training.' Compare chap. 60.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 213------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_213"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 213</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_213_note_1"></a><a href="#page_213_fr_1">1</a> Here first appear the <i>Kh</i>abbaggiya Bhikkhus (the company of the 'six Bhikkhus', with their attendants), the constant and indefatigable evil-doers throughout the whole Vinaya-Pi<i>t</i>aka. Buddhaghosa (on <i>K</i>ullayagga I, 1) says that Pa<i>nd</i>uka and Lohitaka belonged to this company, and also Assa<i>g</i>i and Punabbasu are mentioned as <i>Kh</i>abbaggiyas (see Childers s. v. <i>kh</i>abbaggiyo).</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 215------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_215"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 215</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_215_note_1"></a><a href="#page_215_fr_1">1</a> The case of the novice's committing sexual intercoutse with a Bhikkhunî can have found its place here only by a negligence of the redactor, as it is comprised already in the third of the ten cases (the novice's committing impurity). Buddhaghosa (who of course never admits anything like an inadvertence of the holy Theras by whom the Vinaya is compiled) says that the third case and the tenth are distinguished here, because a person that has simply committed an impurity may receive the ordination, if he is willing to refrain himself in future; whilst a bhikkhunîdûsaka cannot be ordained in any case (see chap. 67).</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_215_note_2"></a><a href="#page_215_fr_2">2</a> Tena kho pana samayena a<i>ññ</i>ataro pa<i>nd</i>ako bhikkhûsu pabba<i>g</i>ito hoti, so dahare dahare bhikkhû upasa<i>m</i>kamitvâ eva<i>m</i> vadeti: etha ma<i>m</i> âyasmanto dûsethâ 'ti. Bhikkhû apasâdenti: nassa pa<i>nd</i>aka, vinassa pa<i>nd</i>aka, ko tayâ attho 'ti. So bhikkhûhi apasâdito mahante mahante moligalle (Buddhaghosa: thûlasarîre) sâma<i>n</i>ere upasa<i>m</i>kamitvâ eva<i>m</i> vadeti: etha ma<i>m</i> âvuso dûsethâ 'ti. Sâma<i>n</i>erâ apasâdenti: nassa pa<i>nd</i>aka, vinassa pa<i>nd</i>aka, ko tayâ at tho 'ti. So sâma<i>n</i>erehi apasâdito hatthibha<i>nd</i>e assabha<i>nd</i>e upasa<i>m</i>kamitvâ eva<i>m</i> vadeti: etha ma<i>m</i> âvuso dûsethâ 'ti. Hatthibha<i>nd</i>â assabha<i>nd</i>â dûsesu<i>m</i>. Te u<i>ggh</i>âyanti khiyanti vipâ<i>k</i>enti: pa<i>nd</i>akâ ime sama<i>n</i>â Sakyaputtiyâ, ye pi imesa<i>m</i> na pa<i>nd</i>akâ te pi pa<i>nd</i>ake dûsenti, eva<i>m</i> ime sabbeva abrahma<i>k</i>ârino 'ti. Assosu<i>m</i> kho bhikkhû hatthibha<i>nd</i>âna<i>m</i> assabha<i>nd</i>âna<i>m</i> u<i>ggh</i>âyantâna<i>m</i> khîyantâna<i>m</i> vipâkentânam. Atha kho te bhikkhû bhagavato etam attha<i>m</i> âro<i>k</i>esu<i>m</i>.</p>

 <!------------------------footnotes page 225------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_225"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 225</font></a>

 <p><a name="page_225_note_1"></a><a href="#page_225_fr_1">1</a> 'Whose fingers are grown together, like bats' wings' (Buddhaghosa).</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_225_note_2"></a><a href="#page_225_fr_2">2</a> Buddhaghosa (Berlin MS.) explains '<i>kh</i>inniriyâpatha' by 'pidhasappi.' We ought to read, no doubt, pî<i>th</i>asappî, which is Sanskrit pî<i>th</i>asarpin, a cripple that is moved on in a rolling chair.</p>

 <a name="footnotes_page_229"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 229</font></a>

 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 233------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_233"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 233</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_233_note_1"></a><a href="#page_233_fr_1">1</a> Here follows the usual complete formula of a <i>ñ</i>atti<i>k</i>atuttha kamma; see chaps. 28. 4-6; 29. 3, &amp;c.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_233_note_2"></a><a href="#page_233_fr_2">2</a> With these sections compare the previous chapters 12, 28 and following, 36 and following. The <i>ñ</i>attis prescribed in this chapter, together with the Three Refuges Formula prescribed in chap. 12, § 4, the whole of chap. 77, and the Four Interdictions form together the current ceremony of ordination (the upasampadâ-kamma-vâ<i>k</i>â) as now still in use in the Order. See the Journal of the Royal Asiatic Society, New Series, VII, p. 1.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 234------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_234"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 234</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_234_note_1"></a><a href="#page_234_fr_1">1</a> I.e., according to Buddhaghosa, repeat to him all the data specified before together, in order that be might be able to give a correct answer when asked about his spiritual age.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 236------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_236"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 236</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_236_note_1"></a><a href="#page_236_fr_1">1</a> The vimokkhas (literally, deliverances) are eight stages of meditation different from the four <i>gh</i>ânas. The characteristics of the different vimokkhas are specified by Childers s. v.</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_236_note_2"></a><a href="#page_236_fr_2">2</a> This temporary expulsion (ukkhepaniyakamma), which is pronounced against Bhikkhus who refuse to see an offence committed by themselves (âpattiyâ adassane), or to atone for such an offence (âpattiyâ appatikamme), or to renounce a false doctrine (pâpikâya di<i>tth</i>iyâ appa<i>t</i>inissagge), must be distinguished from the definitive and permanent expulsion (nâsanâ) which is pronounced against Bhikkhus who have committed a pârâ<i>g</i>ika offence. or in cases like those treated of in chapters 61 seq.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 237------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_237"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 237</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_237_note_1"></a><a href="#page_237_fr_1">1</a> I.e. the sentence of expulsion is abolished; compare the Samanta Pâsâdikâ, ap. Minayeff, Prâtimoksha, p. 92.</p>
 
 <p>
 
 <!------------------------footnotes page 238------------------------>
 
 <a name="footnotes_page_238"><font size="1" color="GREEN">p. 238</font></a>
 
 </p>
 
 <p><a name="page_238_note_1"></a><a href="#page_238_fr_1">1</a> As in §§ 1, 2. Instead of 'Will you see that offence?' and, 'I will see it,' read here: 'Will you atone for that offence?' and, 'I will atone for it.'</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_238_note_2"></a><a href="#page_238_fr_2">2</a> As above. Read here: 'Will you renounce that false doctrine?' and, 'I will renounce it.'</p>
 
 <p><a name="page_238_note_3"></a><a href="#page_238_fr_3">3</a> Here follow some <i>S</i>lokas, probably written in Ceylon, and an elaborate Table of Contents, both of which we leave untranslated. The <i>S</i>lokas are introductory to the Table of Contents (uddâna) and belong to it. A similar Table of Contents is found in the MSS. nearly after all the other Khandhakas.</p>
